text:
This has become a weekend staple at our home. AMAZING BISCUITS!! I finally figured out how to freeze the un-cooked biscuits so now I always have them on hand, but only have to make them once a week.  FYI if you're interested... make the recipe as is, except don't brush them with the butter milk.  Instead put all the un-cook biscuits on a cookie sheet with parchment paper on the tray.  Cover well with plastic wrap and put in the freezer.  Once frozen solid, put into a zip bag and leave in the freezer.  When you want to eat them you can have as many or as few as you need

 Pre-heat the oven to 475F and put frozen biscuits on a parchment paper lined cookie sheet; brush with buttermilk.  Put in the oven for 7 minutes, then without opening the oven turn the oven off and leave the biscuits in for 5 more or until lightly browned (don't open the door during this phase until the 5 min. are up).  add 1 min at a time until lightly browned.  Remove and enjoy!

I use a "vegetable peeler" to grate an entire stick of butter. It works great! So quick and so much easier! Toss those long thin flakes of butter with the dry ingredients to keep them from sticking back together. 
The best buttermilk biscuits I've made since I began my quest for the perfect biscuit. I love the folding technique that left the biscuits with the perfect consistency not to mention flaky. Delicious!

text:
The perfect biscuit recipe! Only change I made was just to use the whole butter stick. The method he uses to work the dough is brilliant. You are not in danger of over working the dough, as that is what makes a dense biscuit, by using the fold and press method. I have made these several times and the taste is wonderful. They bake up light and fluffy.  Two important tips that I have learned in making the perfect biscuit. 
1) use an up and down motion with your biscuit cutter or glass(whichever you use to cut your biscuits). Using a twisting motion causes the edges to seal and you will not get a good rise. 
2) when placing the cut biscuit dough on your baking sheet, place them so they touch one another. This also helps reach a wonderful rise as they bake. 
I have deemed this the definitive biscuit recipe and my search has ended here. As a matter of fact, I'm making these in the morning since the kids, yet again, have another snow day. 
And as always... Enjoy!

text:
Wonderful standard recipe.  There is a certain skill set needed to make good biscuits, so check out the video for tips.  Make sure you touch the dough as LITTLE as possible to yield the most tender and flaky results.  In order to avoid over-working the dough, after everything is mostly combined, I turn out onto a lightly floured surface and pat into a 1" thick round, then cut into wedges (like you would a pizza or pie).  This also avoids any left over scraps.  Once you get the method down, this is a no-fail recipe.

text:
I just made these biscuits and they were wonderful! After reading the reviews I decided to use my food processor since I have very bad hands. I pulsed the dry ingredients 3 times to mix them, then I added the butter which I had cut into small pieces. I pulsed that 4 times and it was crumbly so I added the milk and pulsed 4 times so it would hold together. I removed it and followed the rest of the directions as to patting it out. All in all it was oven ready in 4 minutes. My cookie cutter was only 2 inches so I had to adjust my cook time to 11 minutes. These were very simple to make and had a great taste.

These are the best buiscuits ever!! The texture was perfect and they worked wonderfully with whole wheat flour as well! I ran out of all purpose flour the first time i used this recipe and had to use whole wheat. It was amazing. The difference in texture from the normal ones was...nonexistant. They always turn out wonderfullty and hold shape very well, they don't break or fall apart or crumble when you cut them open. I followed the recipe to a T , remembered not to knead my dough to much(just fold it a few times, made my biscuits a little bigger and thicker than the recipe says and brushed them with buttermilk before baking. These biscuits turn out delicious everytime and i will continue to use this recipe as the whole family loves it!

text:
Made these as directed, but used my food processor. Put the butter and dry ingredients in the freezer overnight, along with the food processor bowl and blade.  Process the butter and dry ingredients just until it resembles slightly wet sand, then drizzle in the buttermilk. To make them more tender (no kneading), I turned the mixture out into a 8x8 baking pan, and used a sharp knife to cut the mixture into 9 equal pieces.  The biscuits came out tall and tender, and perfect for the sausage gravy I had made!!!

userID:
17362447
Display Name:
Pambie
isPro:

photo:
https://images.media-allrecipes.com/userphotos/50x50/5674138.jpg
city:
None
region:
None
country:
None
text:
Years of trial and error when it comes to biscuit making have resulted in perfect buttermilk biscuits - tender, flakey, sky high.  Still I experiment, always wondering if my biscuits could be improved upon. 

So, what are great biscuits? Essentially a fairly common mixture of flour, baking powder, salt, butter - and buttermilk.  The difference in the recipe lies in the handling. I decided to give 'Chef John's' method a whirl. The result? A biscuit that was, yes, tender and flakey, but not nearly as high as the method I normally use and, to my mind (and my tester's) far more dense. I far prefer my method.

Turn the dough out onto your board. Handle as minimally as possible and pat - do not roll - your dough into a rectangle. Cut and place quite close together on your cookie sheet (this ensures a higher biscuit). Brush with a mixture of melted butter, salt, and sugar (caramelizes when the biscuits bake, resulting in a wonderfully crisp top) and bake at 450-475 for 12-15 minutes. Frankly I would go head to head with Chef John in a biscuit competition any day. Bring it.

text:
These have potential!  I really want to rate these 3.5 stars but not quite a 4.  I've been looking for the quintessential biscuit recipe but haven't found it.  The bottoms of these baked to a thick, hard crust.  They weren't burned but just "too done".  They had a nice rise and looked pretty!  When I asked my esteemed panel of judges (husband and son) we all agreed that they "tasted like the ingredients" as in "not quite baked but had just a bit of a raw taste" even though they were thoroughly baked.  They were also lacking a flavor element so I think I will add just a touch of sugar (1 1/2 tsp) next time and see if that helps.  I will also bake them on an airbake cookie sheet to insulate the bottoms.  I used a Silpat (which I love and highly recommend) but I used a standard rimmed baking sheet fearing butter may leak out all over my oven - I've had that happen with other recipes.  Leaking was minimal with these so I think I can use the air insulated sheet.  I made the dough cold by mixing the dry with the butter and freezing it for 24 hours.  The dough stayed beautifully cold while I worked with it and, I believe, it really helped with the rising.  As Martha Stewart says "Make it cold and bake it hot!"  I will try this recipe again with a few adjustments and hope my quest will finally end!

Display Name:

city:

region:
None
country:
None
text:
I have been making biscuits sine I was 10 yrs old (thats over 50 years) and this is the basic recipe that all southerners use. As with most things, practice makes perfect. Couple of pointers that ensure perfect biscuits every time - 1) Use a soft, winter wheat flour such as White Lilly. You can order it on line if its not available in your area. 2) Use self-rising flour and make your life easier by eliminating measuring baking soda, powder and salt. 3) I use a mixture of butter and shortening. Butter gives flavor, shortening gives flakiness. 4 or 5 TBL ice cold butter and 2 or 3 of shortening. 4) Cold,cold,cold! I even keep my flour in the fridge. 5) cut butter and shortening into small cubes and rub it in with your hands. Use a snapping motion with your thumbs and forefingers to flatten and rub it into the flour 6) MOST IMPORTANT- use a sharp biscuit cutter in a straight down and up motion. DO NOT TWIST! Twisting or using a dull cutter (such as a drinking glass) compresses the edges and prevents rising. 7) Lastly use a hot oven. I bake at 450 -500 for 10 min. and brush with melted butter as soon as they are done. There is nothing more satisfying as a perfectly baked, hot biscuit!

text:
This is a simple recipe that has been a hit in my house! The recipe as is gets 5 stars. Make sure that all ingredients are kept cold! I actually sift the flour and use a potato peeler to slice the butter (4tbsp butter & 3tbsp shortening). I put the bowl with flour and butter in the freezer for 15 min as well as the cup of buttermilk. This yields a pretty good rise from the dough. I have tried this with Saco buttermilk blend and they are just as good.

Bye bye old biscuit recipe....hello Chef John's!! These look great, are easy to make, and taste great! I didn't have buttermilk so I poured a little under one tbl. of lemon juice in a measuring cup and poured milk over it to the 1 cup line, then mixed together and left sit for 5 min, then used what the recipe called for. Hey Chef John! Loved these biscuits even if I did change slightly. I used 3 T of lard (I make myself) & 3 T of salted butter. I used the salt called for anyway. text:
As a cook I have made about 30,000 buttermilk biscuits. If they are less than a half inch they won't rise and they will be hocky pucks. If they are too thick even 3/4 of an inch they will be large great looking biscuits but the center won't be done. My oven temperature was 525 for 8 min. Whatever temperature you bake them, when you smell them they are done. If they start to brown on the bottom turn on the broiler on the top and watch them carefully. 30 sec. too long and they can burn. I had a pan of melted butter and a brush waiting for them to come out. To this day I never got tired of them. To cut up the butter I used a cooling rack that was sturdy and about 1/4 inch or less openings. The cut cold butter falls into the flour to be mixed dry before the addition of the liquids. If the butter is cold it will not cling as you shove it through the rack. Takes about 1.5 seconds. The mix seemed to last about 15 min. after that it didn't rise as well. The left over pieces when pressed together can be rolled and will make a few more. If you are making more then recycle leftover bits into the next batch. Dough that has aged a little and is not expected to rise can be mixed with a little more flour and rolled out 1/8 inch thick for some of the best dumplins ever. The dumplin dough will be a little tough to keep its shape. Cut into 1 inch squares. There you have it you can make buttered biscuits, gravy and dumplins out of the same dough. Make fried chicken and invite me over! :-D

What an awesome recipe!! I make the dough exactly as stated, down to rolling it out. At the point where the instructions say to roll it out, I just pat it into a rectangle and cut it into 10 - 12 squares (I don't have a biscuit cutter that size).  They cook up flaky and light.  I do find that I have some flour left over with this amount of buttermilk, even though my dough has some really wet parts.  Probably user error though.  I don't want to overmix and melt the butter.  Overall a great recipe that I will make again and again!  :)

Golden brown hockey pucks, but ....  This morning I made these.  I have a gas stove and it requires electricity to maintain temps, and normally doesn't get hot enough for biscuits.  This morning, no power.  I had the dough made and decided to go ahead and just keep a close eye on them.  Well, long story short, they are fabulous! Light, crumbly, delicious! This was my first time attempting buttermilk biscuits and Im so happy with how they turned out. I subbed 1 cup heavy cream + 1Tbsp vinegar for the buttermilk (3/4 c for dough & the rest for brushing on), added shredded rosemary Manchego cheese (about 1/4 cup, definitely should have added more), shredded frozen butter with cheese shredder, and instead of folding just kneaded a couple minutes in the bowl. WOW are these light and wonderful!!

This is exactly what I was looking for! Wonderful texture, the flavor is perfect for a buttermilk biscuit & so easy to make. I did not bake them on a silicone baking sheet because I don't own one & thought of baking them on parchment paper but decided to bake them on a plain cookie sheet simply because I wanted the bottoms to be a firm crust. Having spent most of my life in the South, I'm very picky when it comes to biscuits - but this recipe quickly became our new standby. It flakes just as well as any of the shortening recipes, but makes it so that you don't have to keep shortening on hand. It pairs well with Southern sausage gravy, and doesn't have the slightly sweet taste that many butter biscuit recipes have, making it ideal for a savory dish.

text:
Man, Chef John knows what he's doing! Everything of his that I make is FABULOUS. This recipe is a bit more work, because everything is cold. I even froze the bowl and all the ingredients except the buttermilk. Instead of cutting in the butter, I grated it and whisked. I did sift the dry ingredients also. I didn't have buttermilk, so I made my own (1 cup milk with 2 T white vinegar, refrigerated overnight, nice and bubbly). To get the most biscuits from the dough without overworking it trying to obtain the "extra" ones, I rolled the dough into a rectangle and then cut square biscuits using a putty knife. I like 'em big, so the yield was only 6. I baked them at 375? for 20 minutes, and they were perfect, evenly golden, with a nice rise. The crust is like biting into a puff pastry or croissant, soft inside but not airy. I will make these again.

text:
We have a dairy allergy so I subbed regular crisco for the butter and coconut creamer with a tablespoon of lemon juice for the buttermilk. These are light and fluffy but not too much so. I did try with butter flavor crisco but in a side by side taste test all family members prefer the ones with regular crisco. I have also rolled and cut the dough and refrigerated overnight or frozen and cooked later. They dont rise quite as high but close and the frozen ones take about 1.5 times the cooking time. I also used the dough to make a biscuit with a sausage baked in the middle and they turned out great. Thank you for a wonderful recipe!

text:
Well I messed this up. It all went well until I accidentally put in 1 full cup of buttermilk. Ooops!  Oh well. I compensated by adding 1.5 tsp of sugar and of course more flour. Then when I was cutting a made it too thin and ended up with 15 biscuits!  Well no wants a flat biscuit so I redid about half of them and recut. Then because I had used up all my buttermilk I brushed with melted butter after they baked. Still came out delicious! The ironic part is the biscuits I didnt remake were a little tough but the ones I remade, and this worked more, came out tender and fluffy!  The opposite of what I would have thought. But both tasted great and my family wont notice. The moral of the story is that this is a forgiving recipe but watch that buttermilk!  By the way adding the sugar gave a slight sweet taste to biscuits as expected and was fantastic! I would recommend that modification.   Southern cooking requires sugar in everything.

city:

text:
I think I'll end up memorizing this recipe from making it multiple times- it's definitely a keeper. I admit I did use a slightly different method when incorporating the butter into the flour mixture, just because it's a technique I'm used to and am comfortable with.  After cutting in the butter for a few minutes with a pastry cutter, when there were still small lumps of butter visible in the "coarse crumb" texture, I switched to using my hands. I picked up handfulls of the mixture and flattened the butter pieces into thin flakes by sliding one hand against the other. The end result is a crumbly mixture with small, flat pieces of butter coated in flour which helps create flaky layers (think rough puff pastry). I also used a full 1/2 cup of butter (because I didn't feel like measuring 7 tbsps).  Turned out great!

text:
My new go-to biscuit recipe.   One thing I did different,  was I almost froze the butter,  then instead of cutting it,  I grated right over the flour mixture with a course cheese grater.    Grate,  combine, grate combine until the butter was incorporated.   This seemed to keep the butter from getting mushed.  Biscuits were 1.5 inches tall after baking.

text:
Great flavor. The dough seemed slightly dry so i added closer to a cup of buttermilk instead of 3/4. The key with biscuits for me is the handling of the dough. I used a food processor with a dough blade to pulse he dry ingredients and butter. I then turned into a shallow bowl and added the buttermilk and mixed gently  but only worked the dough till it just barely came together. It was still crumbly in parts. . turned out onto floured board and pressed into a circle (I didn't make the rectangle) with a wooden spoon dipped in flour.

Also I like biscuits a bit puffier so i only made 8 one inch  biscuits with a round cutter  and placed them touching side to side on the pan.  

The flavor was great. Will make again.

text:
These biscuits have great texture.  They have a slight crispness on the outside with soft layers in the middle.  

I easily turned them vegan by substituting butter for Smart Balance (or any other vegan substitute) and making buttermilk from almond milk.  (Don't skip this step.  You'll need the acid from the vinegar for it to rise properly with the baking soda.)  For the vegan buttermilk, add 1 Tbsp of vinegar to 1 cup of non-dairy, unsweetened milk.  Wait 5-10 minutes and it will thicken and curdle.   With the vegan version, you will still get the flakey texture and buttery taste.  Follow the folding directions to create the flakey layers.  
Thanks Chef John!

text:
My absolute favorite, and now ONLY, biscuit recipe I will ever make. I always have frozen butter anyway, so it's easy to take a sharp knife (carefully, of course) and get it to shred up perfectly. I always add the full stick (8 tbs instead of 7 tbs), and end up cutting the dough in 9 pieces. It's a very yummy, simple recipe to follow. It's really critical to layer as Chef John states - otherwise you end up with dense little hockey pucks instead of fluffy buttery biscuits. Yum, thanks for such a great recipe!!

text:
I liked this recipe. I won't call it incredible but it was good. The biscuits came out with a nice golder color. They were a bit crispy on the outside and soft on the inside. Now, for the people who say that they came out flat, biscuit dough needs to be manipulated the least amount of time. Second, my tip to avoid dryness is to bake them close together. It will work. Bake them in two batches and close to each other and you will have very nice moist edges. I will still continue making the Never Fail Biscuits from this site which delivers an unbelievably delicious soft and fluffy biscuit. I will keep this one for sake of variety as they are crispy on the outside and fluffy buttery on the inside.

text:
Thanks Chef John for a wonderful recipe. I  loved your easy instructions in the video and was able to make them right the first time. Fantastic and is now my go to recipe for biscuits, the only changes I make is to add 1/2 tsp sugar and I brush the cooked biscuits with melted butter vs the milk beforehand. 

Some tips for newbies: you can use a whole stick of butter and use regular milk or cream and just add 2 tsp of vinegar to make buttermilk. I use a food processor to mix ingredients, when I add the buttermilk I only do four pulses before I dump it out on the counter. Make sure to watch the video, the dough is supposed to be ugly looking with clumps of butter and flour and just barely holding together. This is what makes it crusty and flaky later. Yum yum yum!

I've always be hesitant to make biscuits, afraid they wouldn't be flaky enough or bland. Oh my was I wrong. Didn't change a thing and these were great. Didn't use a rolling pin, turned the dough out onto a floured surface and used my fingers to pat our the dough, folded into thirds a few times and just patted the dough. It was so easy and the biscuits were so flaky. They were light and layered due to the folding of the dough. Will definitely make these again and again!!

text:
This recipe and technique is absolutely fool-proof. I love the tri-fold pastry technique when pressing the dough because it produces such great layers in the end product; and, brushing the tops with buttermilk is a perfect way to create a lightly-browned top without making them greasy. I never have buttermilk in the house so I made my own by adding a couple teaspoons of white wine vinegar to milk, and I didn't have any unsalted butter so I used some really good English butter and didn't add any salt. This will be my go-to biscuit recipe from now on!!

https://images.media-allrecipes.com/userphotos/50x50/5614261.jpg
city:
None
region:
None
country:
None
text:
Whenever I have made biscuits in the past they are always beautiful but inedible - like lovely rocks. I sliced and chilled the butter just like Chef John said.  I cut in the butter with my pastry blender without twisting, as another reviewer suggested. I stirred in the buttermilk until just combined - until I didn't see anymore pools of buttermilk - and stopped stirring. I followed Chef John's instructions on folding the rectangle into thirds, then gently rolled out the dough. The only change I made was I cut the biscuit dough with a knife because I was afraid to manipulate the dough scraps after using a biscuit cutter.

I baked only two biscuits because that was all I needed. I froze the remaining biscuits before baking as another reviewer suggested. To cook the frozen biscuits, I preheated the oven to 425F, brushed buttermilk on the tops of each, and baked for 15 minutes. The frozen biscuits were just as light and yummy as the fresh ones. I placed the frozen biscuits so that they were touching, and they did rise higher than those that didn't touch. My husband loves biscuits and gravy. My gravy is killer - now my biscuits are, too. Thank you, Chef John!

text:
AWESOME!!!!!!  These are the best biscuits I've ever had!  The folding technique Chef John teaches is just awesome.  It's one of those things you just have to see done and then you get it!  I did make a few changes, I used a full 1/2 cup of FROZEN butter, grated with a cheese grater.  I also added 2 tbsp. of sugar.  I've also found you get the best rise if just after you mix it up, you GENTLY pat it into a rough ball or mound, wrap it in plastic wrap sprayed with vegetable spray and then pop it in the fridge for a couple of hours. Then pull it out, do the folding technique, then cut out your biscuits.    Oh I also brushed the tops with melted salted butter (no substitutions) before and after they were baked.  I then whipped up some breakfast sausage gravy and WOW!  Best biscuits and gravy I've ever had.  These biscuits are tender and then crunchy in just the right spots.  Even though they are tender, they do hold up to gravy.   If you can make a good pie crust by hand, you can do this.  It's a very similar technique when cutting in the butter and adding the liquid.  You want to toss it together, NOT stir.  You will feel like a chef when you make these awesome little babies!!!  One final tip, if you make your mound to cut from rectangular and then cut square biscuits, you don't have any waste and they ALL turn out with the same awesome texture!

text:
I didn't make any changes and would make again. 
Just a few tips of my own for those that may have problems: Keep the butter cold so the fat doesn't break down (so don't use your hands). Don't overwork your dough or you'll get the tough, hard biscuits you are trying to avoid.  Don't roll your dough with a pin, but press it out with your hands. Don't twist your cutter when you cut because this will seal the edges and you won't get a good lift, making a denser, flatter, and tougher biscuit. Finally, don't ever use a glass or cup to cut biscuits because this too will seal the edges and prevent the flakiness and lightness that you're looking for.
As for the recipe, I LOVE that it can stand on it's own and tastes like a great biscuit.  Most biscuits are used as flavor holders.  For example, eating a grape jelly biscuit with a "whop it on the counter biscuit" or other recipes, you basically eat grape jelly held together with flavorless dough.  This recipe, you'd get a great biscuit flavor with grape jelly added. The flavor of this biscuit is devine!  LOVE IT!

text:
I have to say the video Chef John provided made a believer out of me for the method to produce flakey LAYERED biscuits. I know now his recommendation of stopping the mixing of the ingredients before you feel it's time, and allowing the final mixing to be completed during the folding over process is the key to lighter, flakier biscuits. I was skeptical about stopping the mixing in the bowl before I felt the dough to be ready, but I forged ahead as per the video......and the dough turned out exactly as John said it would.  I also used my food processor to pulse the ice cold butter into the dry ingredients, taking care to stop while the bits of butter were still visible. The ingredient measurements and baking times were spot on. The biscuits came out tender and flakey with layers of buttery goodness. Hubby loved them hot and once cooled,  they keep well to split and toast the next day.  These are easy to make and have become "My" biscuit recipe.

I mixed together the dry ingredients and butter, then froze it. When I was ready to have biscuits, I took out half the dry mix, added half the buttermilk, and baked a small batch. These biscuits are OUTSTANDING.  I made them last night and was quite pleased with the results.  I watched the video and followed the recipe exactly as written; with NO modifications or changes.

I used real buttermilk, not the milk with vinegar method or the powdered stuff.  I've used both of these types of buttermilk before but real buttermilk just makes a world of difference.  And best of all; you can FREEZE the leftover buttermilk into any amount of single size serving you need.  Just thaw the amount of buttermilk you need for your next recipe.

My biscuits turned out exactly as pictured; FLAKY and TALL.   

This will be my one and only biscuit recipe.

THANK YOU...THANK YOU...THANK YOU Chef John

NOTE: To anyone that thought these biscuits were dry or too salty, make sure you are using unsalted butter and make sure your oven has been calibrated. 
I usually make biscuits with self-rising flour because every time I try  making them with baking powder (different recipes) they always seem heavy and flavorless. Today I didn't have any self-rising so I decided to give it one more shot. I knew I could trust Chef John! These biscuits are moist, light, flaky, and tasty. I followed the recipe to the letter. Next time I'll add just a tad more buttermilk  because the dough still tried to crumble even after the third fold.

text:
These are nice and fluffy biscuits. I make a few minor modifications, primarily due to availability of ingredients where we live. Buttermilk is practically nonexistent in our corner of the globe, so I use whole yogurt without any added sugar in the same proportion. I also add some grated cheese right after I blend in the butter, just to add a bit of character. About the only thing I ever liked at Red Lobster was their biscuits (way back in the day -- never go there anymore) and likes the little bit of cheese flavor they had. I also had some incredible biscuits several years ago at a place called the Blue Plate Cafe in Memphis. They were light, fluffy, and melt-in-your-mouth good. These remind me a great deal of those. I've had numerous requests for them over the years. Wonderful recipe.

Great recipe! I just made these this morning. I didn't change anything except as you can see the shape. I didn't have a round cutter so I used a knife and just cut square shapes. The extra dough became my big biscuit. I used my Ninja blender to cut the dough into the flour. I was worried that it wouldn't do well but because the butter was frozen it came out perfect. I just did really quick pulse. I mixed the buttermilk in the bowl with the flour mixture. Flaky on the inside and just a little crunch on the outside Delicious. I will definitely make again and also recommend.

text:
I had to make this twice to get it right. The first time the dough was too dry. The second time I increased the amount of buttermilk to 1 cup. Also, on the second try, instead of cutting up the butter, I used a cheese grater and grated the cold butter into the dry ingredients. If you use this method, be sure to coat the butter shreds with the dry ingredients so they don't clump together. Second try was much better! I still need more practice, though.

text:
These were wonderful. As someone who has never done a biscuit more complex than a Bisquick drop biscuit, I thought I was being ambitious by trying this homemade biscuit for the first time. I'm pretty experienced in the kitchen but recently trying to expand by doing things like my own pie crusts and now biscuits (things that can be ruined being overworked)...it turned out fantastic! My only word of advice it not to fear your dough being under-worked. Chef John's video is a wonderful guide. I always freeze half my butter when I buy it (Costco) and it works well when doing biscuits (AND pie crusts for that matter). They didn't seem to rise much but I made them pretty small, next time I will double the recipe and roll my dough out thicker. They were pretty flaky and the flavor was absolutely wonderful. I made them for the hubby, and my 10 month old daughter, and when my older kids tried them, they demanded more. Unfortunately we ate them all, haha these will be a hot commodity around my house. (*EDIT: Made them again, and this time I just made 6 large biscuits--I cut the dough with a knife ad just made square biscuits to eliminate having the leftover tough ones--and it was wonderful!)

country:
None
text:
These turned out really well each time I've made them. Make sure you don't overwork the dough, or the texture will suffer, as with any biscuit. It should also be noted that self rising flour works wonderfully in this recipe if you don't have plain on hand! If they're turning out dry, you should have added just a bit more buttermilk to your dough. A big part of making biscuits is getting the dough just right and there's not really a way to tell you how to do that through text. You just have to practice, practice practice until you find your personal "sweet spot" where your dough isn't too dry, nor too sticky/wet. It also really helps if you start out with your butter frozen before cutting it in with a hand held pastry blender.

text:
Followed recipe exactly (video is helpful). Rose beautifully; tasted great; quick to make. Tips: don't overcook. In my oven, at least, 15 minutes is too long, especially if you space the biscuits an inch or more apart. If they are touching in the pan they will be softer. Also, if you keep unsalted butter in the freezer all the time it will last virtually forever and be ready for any baking activity that requires super cold butter like this one. Finally, as another reviewer said, cutting the butter into the flour is much easier with a food processor. Process for about 20 seconds. I will definitely make this recipe again.

text:
Thank you Chef John for the recipe and the video. I do not have a pastry cutter and the last time I attempted to make scratch biscuits, it took forever to cut the butter in with a fork. This time, I took another reviewer's suggestion and used a grater to grate the very cold butter into the flour, and then I used a fork to just work it in until it resembled course crumbs. Worked perfectly. I followed the rest of the instructions exactly and the biscuits came out delicious. I made them last night and my husband has asked for them again tonight. If you're a novice to biscuit-making like me, take a few minutes and watch the video first. This is definitely a winning recipe.

text:
Just made these for my family's breakfast. I didn't have any buttermilk, so I used a priceless tip from an allrecipes reviewer that I read long ago. Pour a full Tablespoon of white vinegar into your 1 cup measuring cup, then fill the rest with milk. Stir gently, wait 5 minutes, and you have buttermilk substitute! Everything else I followed exactly to the recipe, and they were great! Thank you Chef John...p.s. The video is very helpful to watch first if you haven't made biscuits before or if it's been a while.

text:
If these got any more perfect, I'd eat them 24/7.  I was put off at first by the gooey dough but it worked up GREAT in the end.  I used sour milk instead of buttermilk with obviously great results.  I also mixed the dough in my food processor -- cuts the butter in wonderfully!  I used the first batch to make breakfast pockets, patting blobs of dough onto the baking sheet into rounds, topping with meat / cheese then patting on another blob of dough until the edges sealed. I've used whole wheat flour as well -- a bit dry but good with jam.  I've also used an ice cream scoop to portion the dough.  Making a batch now, to see how it works into cinnamon rolls...

I cooked half of the recipe.  I didn't have buttermilk on hand, so, I used 1/4 cup plus 2 tablespoons of milk and added 1/2 tablespoon of vinegar.  I let it sit for about 5 minutes.  I followed the recipe as is but instead of brushing buttermilk on the top of the biscuits, I dipped them in butter.  Update:  I used to use a biscuit cutter to make the biscuits but because of the round shape I was losing a lot of the dough.  When I make half of the recipe, I now cut the biscuits in thirds.  They end up in log form instead of traditional biscuit form but they still taste delicious.  Bake for 13 minutes.

text:
Best biscuits I've ever made! Maybe the best I ever et. It should be a bit labor intensive (if you're doing it right). The frozen butter is difficult to cut into the flour, but well worth the effort. Invest in a good pastry cutter; your wrists will thank you.

Also, I usually prepare the dry ingredients and butter the night before, then leave in the coldest spot in the fridge for morning. That way all I have to do before breakfast is add the buttermilk, knead/fold, and cut.
